Preference of Date vs. Absolute Ownership in Claims
Explaining the dispute among Hanafi imams on date and absolute ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the preference between a dated and an absolute ownership claim, and explain the legal reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a date does not automatically give priority over an absolute ownership claim.

When two claimants present evidence for the same property, one with a specific date and the other with an absolute claim of ownership, how does a judge decide? The great Hanafi imams disagreed on this matter, each providing nuanced reasoning based on certainty, comprehensiveness, and the nature of claims. This lesson unpacks their arguments and the practical consequences for a qadi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
First, recall that Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad each hold a distinct view on the preference between a dated claim and an absolute (undated) claim. Write down each imam's position.
2
Analyze the proofs of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad
Examine the rational proofs (aql) offered by each imam. Abu Yusuf relies on certainty (yaqeen) and analogizes to purchase claims. Imam Muhammad argues from comprehensiveness, including rights to offspring and profits. Contrast the two reasoning styles.
Pay attention to the analogy (qiyas) to purchase – note how the author later rebuts it.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's middle ground
Abu Hanifa argues that the date neither proves priority nor is it irrelevant; the possibility that the absolute claim is earlier cancels the benefit of the date, leaving both equal. Grasp why he rejects both extremes.
This view treats date as non-decisive – a unique Hanafi position.
4
Examine the rebuttal of Abu Yusuf's analogy
The passage explicitly addresses the analogy to purchase (bilaaf shiraa). Abu Yusuf says a dated purchase claim always wins over an undated one. The author explains why purchase is different: purchase is a recent act, so without a date it is attributed to the nearest time, making the dated claim earlier with certainty. But in ownership claims, absolute ownership is not a recent act – it can extend back indefinitely.
Understanding the distinction between ownership and purchase is crucial for applying the correct ruling.
5
Explore the 32 scenarios framework
The benefit (fa'idah) at the end lists the combinatorial logic: 3 types of claim (absolute, purchase, inheritance) × 3 possession situations × 4 date scenarios = 32 cases. This systematizes when each position applies. Trace one example from each combination to see how the dispute fits into the broader theory.
This taxonomy is found in Kifayah and helps a qadi handle all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں۔ کہ تاریخ والا ہیتہ اولی ہوگا۔ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک جس نے مطلق چھوڑا ہے وہ اولی ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ جس نے تاریخ بیان نہیں کی وہ اقلیت ملک کا مدعی ہے۔ بدلیل آنکہ جو شخص ملک مطلق کا مدعی ہو تو اس مال سے جوزوائد ( متصله و منفصلہ جیسے اولا دوا کساب) حاصل ہوں اُن کا مستحق وہی ہوتا ہے۔ نیز اگر یہ چیز کسی نے اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر کے لے لی تو پچھلی بیع کرنے والے لوگ اپنے اپنے بائع سے برابر ثمن پھیرتے جائیں گے۔ معلوم ہوا کہ ملک مطلق اقویٰ ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب تاریخ مذکور ہو تو ملک بالیقین اسی وقت سے ثابت ہو جاتی ہے۔ اور ملک مطلق کے دعوئی میں یہ احتمال ہے کہ وہ اول نہ ہو۔ پس یقین کو احتمال پر ترجیح ہوگی جیسے اگر دو مدعیوں میں سے ہر ایک نے خرید کا دھلا کر کے اپنی اپنی گواہی میں تاریخ بیان کی تو جس کی تاریخ مقدم ہو وہ اولی ہوتا ہے۔ قوله ولابي حنيفة ان التاريخ امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہاں تاریخ کی مزاحمت میں یہ احتمال قائم ہے کہ شاید یہ مقدم نہ ہو۔ یعنی تاریخ کے وقت سے ملکیت اگر چہ یقینی ہے لیکن ملک مطلق کے مدعی سے اس کا مقدم ہونا یقینی نہیں۔ لہذا تاریخ کا اعتبار ساقط ہو گیا۔ پس ایسارہ گیا کہ گویا دونوں نے بلا تاریخ ملک مطلق پر بنے قائم کئے۔ اور اس میں دونوں کے مساوی ہونے کا حکم ہوتا ہے تو یہاں بھی یہی ہوگا۔ قوله بخلاف الشراء ... امام ابو یوسف کے قول كما لو ادَّعيا الشراء" کا جواب ہے کہ بخلاف دعوی شراء کے یعنی جب ایک نے مطلق مع تاریخ کا اور دوسرے نے خرید بلا تاریخ کا دعوی کیا تو یہاں تاریخ مقدم ہے۔ کیونکہ خرید تو ایک فعل جدید واقع ہوتا ہے تو بدون تاریخ کے وہ سب سے قریب وقت کی جانب منسوب ہو گا۔ پس تاریخ والے کو اس پر ترجیح ہوگی۔ مثلاً ایک شخص نے ۱۲۸۰ھ میں اپنے باپ سے ایک گھوڑا میراث پایا جس کو کسی نے چرا کر ۱۲۸۰ھ میں زید کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا۔ تو یہاں خرید اصل مالک کی ملکیت کے بعد واقع ہوئی۔ اب اگر اصل مالک یہ دعوے کرے کہ میں نے اس کو ۱۲۸۰ھ میں میراث پایا ہے۔ اور زید بلا تاریخ یہ کہے کہ میں نے خریدا ہے تو حکم تاریخ والے کے لئے ہوگا۔ کیونکہ بیع تو اس کے ایک روز بعد بھی ہو سکتی ہے۔
فائدہ جب دو آدمی عین شئی کا دعوی کریں تو یہ تین حال سے خالی نہیں۔ دعویٰ ملک مطلق کا ہوگا یا شرا کا یا ارث کا۔ ان میں سے ہر ایک کی پھر تین صورتیں ہیں۔ مال عین ان میں سے کسی ایک کے قبضہ میں ہوگا۔ یا دونوں کے یا شخص ثالث کے۔ ان میں سے ہر ایک کی پھر چار صورتیں ہیں۔ دونوں نے کوئی تاریخ بیان نہیں کی ہوگی۔ یا کوئی ایک ہی تاریخ بیان کی ہوگی۔ یا ایک تاریخ مقدم اور دوسرے کی مؤخر ہوگی۔ یا ان میں سے صرف ایک نے تاریخ بیان کی ہوگی دوسرے نے نہیں۔ پس یہ ۳۲ صورتیں ہیں۔ وقد عرف ذلك في موضعه (كفايه)
Abu Yusuf says: The one with a date is preferable. And according to Imam Muhammad, the one who left [the claim] absolute (without date) is preferable, because the one who did not mention a date claims a lesser degree of ownership. The proof is that the person claiming absolute ownership is entitled to the offspring and profits (both connected and separate, like children and earnings) from that property. Also, if someone takes the thing after proving his entitlement, previous buyers will recover their price from their sellers. So absolute ownership is stronger. Abu Yusuf's proof: When a date is mentioned, ownership is definitely established from that time. In a claim of absolute ownership, there is a possibility that it is earlier. So certainty is given preference over probability, like if two claimants each claim purchase and give a date in their evidence, the one with the earlier date is preferable. Abu Hanifa's proof: There is the possibility that the date may not be earlier. Although ownership from the time of the date is certain, it is not certain that it is earlier than the claimant of absolute ownership. So the date loses its validity, and both are considered as if they gave evidence without date, and they are considered equal. Rebuttal of Abu Yusuf's analogy of purchase: The case of purchase is different. When one claims absolute ownership with a date and the other claims purchase without a date, the one with the date is preferred, because purchase is a recent act and without a date it is attributed to the nearest time. So the one with the date has precedence. For example, someone inherited a horse from his father in 1280 AH, and someone stole it and sold it to Zayd in 1280 AH. The purchase occurred after the original owner's ownership. If the original owner claims he inherited it in 1280 AH, and Zayd claims he bought it without a date, judgment is for the one with the date, because the sale could have happened a day later. Benefit: When two persons claim a specific thing, there are three possibilities: claim of absolute ownership, purchase, or inheritance. Each has three subcases regarding possession: in one's hand, in both's, or in a third's. Each then has four subcases regarding dates: both give no date, one gives a date, one date is earlier and the other later, or only one gives a date. Thus 32 scenarios. And that is known in its place (Kifayah).
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the preference between a claimant who provides a specific date for his ownership and another who claims absolute ownership without a date. Abu Yusuf argues that the date creates certainty and should be preferred, analogizing to purchase claims where a date is decisive. Imam Muhammad argues that absolute ownership is stronger because it implies a longer and more comprehensive right, including offspring and profits, and due to practical consequences in subsequent transactions. Abu Hanifa takes a middle position: the date does not necessarily prove priority over the absolute claim, so both are treated equally. The author then rebuts Abu Yusuf's analogy to purchase, showing that purchase is a recent act, so date is more meaningful there. The passage concludes with a systematic enumeration of 32 possible scenarios involving type of claim, possession, and date, establishing the theoretical framework for such disputes.

Key Terms
تاریخ (tārīkh): A specific date mentioned in evidence for ownership
ملک مطلق (mulk muṭlaq): Absolute claim of ownership without specifying a time
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two witnesses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, why is the claimant of absolute ownership preferred over the one with a specific date?
A) Because absolute ownership includes profits and offspring
B) Because date creates uncertainty
C) Because absolute ownership is easier to prove
D) Because the hadith supports it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a date does not automatically give priority over an absolute ownership claim; the ikhtilaf among the imams requires the judge to choose carefully based on the preferred view.

Preference of the Possessor in Birth Evidence
Ruling when both parties establish evidence on birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the possessor's evidence prevails when both parties establish equal proof of an animal's birth, and explain the legal reasoning and hadith basis.

Core Idea
When both parties have equal proof of birth, possession tips the scale — the possessor wins.

In disputes over animal ownership, both parties might bring evidence that the animal was born in their possession. Since birth is a one-time event, possession alone doesn't prove it, so both evidences are equal. However, the Hanafi school holds that the possessor's evidence is strengthened by his physical control, giving him priority. This ruling, supported by a hadith, is the majority view within the school, contrary to a minority opinion by Isa ibn Aban.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Two parties claim an animal; both present evidence (bayyinah) that it was born (nitāj) in their possession. The animal is currently held by one (the qābiḍ).
2
Understand the nature of the evidence
Birth (nitāj) is a non-recurring event that possession does not inherently prove. Hence, both evidences are equal in their probative value.
3
Apply the possession advantage
Since the evidences are equal, the possessor's evidence gains preponderance through his existing control (yad). Judgment is given in his favor.
Rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) is cited: possession adds weight to the possessor's testimony.
4
Examine the textual proof (dalīl naqlī)
The hadith from Dar Qutni: the Prophet (ﷺ) awarded a she-camel to the possessor when both brought evidence of birth. This serves as a precedent.
Hadith thubut is zanni, but dalāla is qat‘ī (clear implication).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Isa ibn Aban held that both evidences cancel and the animal remains with the possessor without a judicial ruling (i.e., no qaḍā‘). The majority (jumhūr) differ, holding that a judicial decision is made in favor of the possessor.
Practical fruit: in the majority view, the possessor does not take an oath; in Isa's view, he would.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قابض اور غیر قابض دونوں نے بینہ قائم کئے پیدائش پر تو قابض اولی ہے
قَالَ وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجُ وَصَاحِبُ الْيَدِ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بَيْنَةً عَلَى النِّتَاجِ فَصَاحِبُ الْيَدِ أَوْلَى لِأَنَّ الْبَيِّنَةَ قَامَتْ عَلى مَا لَاتَدُلُّ عَلَيْهِ الْيَدُ فَاسْتَوَيَا وَتَرَبَّحَتْ بَيْنَةُ ذِي الْيَدِ بِالْيَدِ فَيُقْضَى لَهُ وَهَذَا هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ خِلَافًا لِمَا يَقُولُهُ عِيسَى بْنُ أَيَّانٌ أَنَّهُ تَتَهَاتَرَ الْبَيِّنَتَانِ وَيُتْرَكُ فِي يَدِهِ لَا عَلَى طَرِيقِ الْقَضَاءِ
ترجمہ اگر بینہ قائم کیا قابض و غیر قابض میں سے ہر ایک نے پیدائش پر تو قابض اولیٰ ہے کیونکہ بینہ اس پر قائم ہوا ہے۔ جس پر قبضہ دلات نہیں کرتا۔ پس دونوں برابر ہو گئے اور قابض کا مبینہ قبضہ کی وجہ سے راج ہوا تو اس کے لئے حکم کیا جائے گا۔ یہی صحیح ہے۔ برخلاف اس کے جو عیسی بن ابان کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں بینے ساقط ہوں گے۔ اور چیز قابض کے قبضہ میں غیر قضا کے طور پر چھوڑ دی جائے گی۔
تشریح... قوله وان اقام الخارج شخص خارج اور قابض دونوں نے ملک کے ایسے سب پر بہینہ قائم کیا۔ جومکر واقع نہیں ہوتا۔ ایک ہی بار ہوتا و ثمرة الخلاف انما تظهر في حق تحليف ذي اليد و عدمه فعنده يحلف ذواليد للخارج و عندنا لا يحلف
ہمارے نزدیک یہی صحیح ہے۔ عام مشائخ اسی طرف گئے ہیں۔ عیسی بن ابان اس کے خلاف ہیں۔ وہ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں پینے ساقط ہو جائیں گے۔ اور جس چیز میں جھگڑا ہے وہ قابض کے قبضہ میں بدستور چھوڑ دی جائے گی نہ کہ بطور حکم قضا۔
قوله عیسی بن ابان ... یہ علماء اصول میں سے ہیں اور قابلِ اعتماد فقیہ ہیں۔ ابن مالک کا بیان ہے کہ یہ پہلے اصحاب حدیث میں سے تھے۔ پھر ان پر رائے کا غلبہ ہو گیا۔ انہوں نے علم فقہ امام محمد سے حاصل کیا ہے۔ اور اس۲۳ھ میں وفات پائی ہے۔
ہماری نقلی دلیل دار قطنی کی روایت جابر بن عبد اللہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے ایک اونٹنی کا دعویٰ کیا اور بینہ سے ثابت کیا کہ یہ میری ہے میرے پاس جنی ہے قابض نے بھی اسی طرح بینہ سے ثابت کیا تو آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اونٹنی قابض کو دے دی۔
قوله لان السنة قامت . بیماری عقلی دلیل ہے کہ مذکورہ گواہی ایسی چیز پر قائم ہوئی ہے جس پر قبضہ دلالت نہیں کرتا تو گواہی میں دونوں برابر ہو گئے۔ پھر قابض کے بینہ کو قبضہ کی وجہ سے ترجیح ہے تو حکم اس کے لئے ہوگا۔ یہی صحیح ہے۔
If both the outsider and the possessor each establish evidence on birth, then the possessor is preferable. He said: And if the outsider and the possessor each establish evidence on the offspring (birth), the possessor is more worthy, because the evidence is established on something that possession does not indicate. So they become equal, and the evidence of the possessor gains an advantage through possession, so judgment is for him. This is the correct view, contrary to what Isa ibn Aban says: that both evidences cancel out and the thing is left in his hand not by way of judgment. Translation: If both the possessor and non-possessor establish evidence on birth, the possessor is more worthy because the evidence is on something that possession does not indicate. So they become equal, and the possessor's evidence becomes preponderant due to possession, so judgment is for him. This is correct, contrary to Isa ibn Aban who says both evidences fall and the thing is left in his hand without judgment. Commentary: His statement 'and if the outsider establishes...' A person outside and the possessor both establish evidence on a cause that does not recur, that occurs only once. The fruit of the difference appears regarding whether the possessor takes an oath. According to him (Isa), the possessor takes an oath to the outsider; according to us, he does not. Our textual proof is the narration of Dar Qutni from Jabir ibn Abdullah that a man claimed a she-camel and established evidence that it was his and born with him; the possessor also similarly established evidence, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave the she-camel to the possessor. And his statement 'because the evidence is established...' is a rational proof: the testimony is established on something that possession does not indicate, so they are equal in testimony. Then the possessor's evidence is given preference due to possession, so judgment is for him. This is what is correct.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling when two parties claim an animal and both bring evidence that it was born in their possession. The key point is that birth (نتاج) is a one-time event that possession alone does not prove; therefore, both evidences are equal. However, since the possessor already has physical control, his evidence is strengthened by his possession, so he wins. This is supported by a hadith from Dar Qutni where the Prophet gave the animal to the possessor. The text also refutes Isa ibn Aban, who argued that both evidences cancel and the thing stays with the possessor without a judicial decision. The commentary explains the practical implication: according to the correct view, the possessor does not need to take an oath, whereas in Isa's view he would.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitāj): Birth of an animal; a one-time event that indicates ownership
قابض (qābiḍ): The person in physical possession of the disputed item
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, typically the testimony of two male witnesses
ترجیح (tarjīḥ): Preference or preponderance given to one evidence over another due to an external factor
ثمرہ الخلاف (thamarah al-khilāf): The practical difference resulting from a scholarly disagreement

Checkpoint

B) Because possession gives an advantage to his evidence
C) Because he has a valid purchase document
D) Because the outsider's evidence is invalid

Key Takeaway
When two parties bring equal proof of an animal's birth, the Hanafi rule uses possession as a tiebreaker to award the animal to the possessor, supported by the Prophet's practice.

Birth Evidence Prevails Over Absolute Ownership and Non-Recurring Causes
Ruling on evidence priority between birth and ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that birth evidence takes priority over absolute ownership and identify which causes of ownership are classified as non-recurring.

Core Idea
Birth evidence proves prior ownership; non-recurring causes like weaving and milking are treated like birth, but recurring causes like rent and planting are treated like absolute ownership.

In court disputes over possession, evidence of birth (nitāj) is a powerful proof because it demonstrates earlier ownership. This lesson explains how the Hanafi school weighs birth evidence against claims of absolute ownership, and how the same principle extends to other non-recurring causes such as weaving, milking, and cheese-making. You will learn to distinguish non-recurring from recurring causes and understand the procedures when new evidence emerges after judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline scenario
Two people both claim ownership from the same third party. One establishes evidence of birth in his own presence. The ruling treats this as equivalent to evidence of birth in his own hand (i.e., as if he possessed the animal at birth).
This scenario is explicitly covered in the first sentence.
2
Compare birth evidence with absolute ownership evidence
When one party presents absolute ownership evidence and the other presents birth evidence, the person with birth wins. Reason: birth establishes priority in time—the animal was first in his ownership, so the other must have received it from him.
Priority is the key concept (أولية الملك).
3
Extend to other non-recurring causes
The same rule applies to any cause of ownership that occurs only once: weaving (if the cloth is not rewoven), cotton spinning, milking, cheese-making, felt-making, goat hair, and shearing wool. These are all in the meaning of birth.
Non-recurring = occurs once and then the object changes form.
4
Distinguish recurring causes
Recurring causes (e.g., rent, building, planting, grain cultivation) are treated like absolute ownership: the outsider (non-possessor) wins if they present evidence. These can happen repeatedly and do not indicate priority.
Recurring = can be done multiple times on the same object.
5
Handle post-judgment scenarios
If a third party later brings birth evidence after a judgment based on birth, judgment is given to the third party unless the original winner re-establishes his evidence. Similarly, a person judged against for absolute ownership can overturn the judgment by later presenting birth evidence, because the first judgment was based on ijtihad and the new evidence is like a clear text (naṣṣ).
Birth evidence is considered stronger and can overturn earlier judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ تَلَقَّى كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا الْمِلْكَ مِنْ رَجُلٍ وَاقَامَ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى النِّتَاجِ عِنْدَهُ فَهُوَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ إِقَامَتِهَا عَلَى النِّتَاجِ فِي يَدِ نَفْسِهِ وَلَوْ أَقَامَ اَحَدُهُمَا الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ وَالْآخَرُ عَلَى النِّتَاجِ فَصَاحِبُ النِّتَاجِ أَوْلَى أَيُّهُمَا كَانَ لِأَنَّ بِبَيِّنَتِهِ قَامَتْ عَلَى أَوَّلِيَةِ الْمِلْكِ فَلَا يَثْبُتُ الْمِلْكُ لِلْآخَرَ إِلَّا بِالتَّلَقِى مِنْ جِهَتِهِ وَكَذَلِكَ إِذَا كَانَ الدَّعْوَى بَيْنَ خَارِجِينَ فَبَيِّنَةُ النِّتَاجِ أَولَى لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَ لَوْ قَضَى بِالنِّتَاجِ لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ ثُمَّ أَقَامَ ثَالِثُ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى النِّتَاحِ يُقْضَى لَهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُعِيدَهَا ذُو الْيَدِ لِاَنَّ الثَّالِثَ لَمْ يُصِرُ مَقْضِيًّا عَلَيْهِ بِتِلْكَ الْقَضِيَّةِ وَكَذَا الْمَقْضِيَ عَلَيْهِ بِالْمِلْكِ الْمُطْلَقِ إِذَا أَقَامَ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى النِّتَاجِ تُقْبَلُ وَيُنْقَضُ الْقَضَاءُ بِهِ لِأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ النَّضِ وَالْاَوَّلُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْإِجْتِهَادِ قَالَ وَكَذَلِكَ النَّسْجُ فِي القِيَابِ الَّتِي لَا تُنْسَجُ إِلَّا مَرَّةً كَغَزْلِ الْقُطْنِ وَكَذَلِكَ كُلُّ سَبَبٍ فِي الْمِلْكِ لَا يَتَكَرَّرُ لِأَنَّهُ فِي مَعْنَى النِّتَاجِ كَحَلْبِ الْلَبَنِ وَاتِّخَاذِ الْجُبْنِ وَاللَّبَدِ وَالْمَرْعِزَّى وَجَرُّ الصُّوفِ وَإِنْ كَانَ يَتَكَرَّرُ قُضِيَ بِهِ لِلْخَارِجِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمِلْكِ الْمُطْلَقِ وَهُوَ مِثْلُ الْخَرِ وَالْبِنَاءِ وَالْغَرْسِ وَزَرَاعَةِ الْحِنْطَةِ وَالْحُبُوبِ فَإِنْ أَشْكَلَ يُرْجَعُ إِلَى أَهْلِ الْخَبَرَةِ لِأَنَّهُمْ أَعْرَفُ بِهِ فَإِنْ أَشْكَلَ عَلَيْهِمْ قُضِيَ بِهِ لِلْخَارِجِ لِاَنَّ الْقَضَاءَ بِبَيِّنَتِهِ هُوَ الْأَصْلُ وَالْعُدُولُ عَنْهُ بِخَبَرِ النِّتَاجِ فَإِذَا لَمْ يَعْلَمْ يُرْجَعُ إِلَى الْأَصْلِ.
If each of them received ownership from a man and establishes evidence of birth in his presence, it is like establishing evidence of birth in his own hand. If one establishes evidence of ownership and the other of birth, the one with birth is more worthy, whoever he may be, because his evidence establishes the priority of ownership, so the other's ownership cannot be proven except by receiving from him. Similarly, if the claim is between two outsiders, the evidence of birth is more worthy for what we have said. If judgment is given on birth to the possessor, then a third person establishes evidence on birth, judgment is given to him, unless the possessor re-establishes, because the third was not under the judgment of that case. Similarly, if the one judged against for absolute ownership establishes evidence on birth, it is accepted and the judgment is overturned, because it is like an error and the first is like ijtihad. He said: Similarly, weaving in garments that are not woven except once, like spinning cotton. Similarly, every cause of ownership that does not recur, because it is in the meaning of birth, like milking, making cheese, felt, goat hair, and shearing wool. If it recurs, judgment is for the outsider, like absolute ownership, such as rent, building, planting, and cultivation of wheat and grains. If it is ambiguous, recourse is to experts, because they are more knowledgeable. If it is ambiguous to them, judgment is for the outsider, because judgment by his evidence is the original principle, and deviation from it by news of birth; if not known, return to the original.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage extends the rule of birth evidence to other non-recurring causes and compares them with claims of absolute ownership. Key principles: (1) If both claim from a third person and one proves birth at his place, it is same as claiming birth in his own hand. (2) Birth evidence prevails over absolute ownership because it establishes priority. (3) If a judgment is based on birth, a third party can later challenge with birth evidence, and the earlier judgment is reversed. (4) Non-recurring causes (like weaving, milking, cheese making, wool shearing) are treated like birth. Recurring causes (like rent, building, planting) are treated like absolute ownership and favor the outsider (non-possessor). In case of ambiguity, experts are consulted; if still unclear, the default is judgment for the outsider.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitāj): Birth (of an animal); a non-recurring cause of ownership
سبب لا يتكرر (sabab lā yatakarrar): A non-recurring cause of ownership; occurs only once
سبب يتكرر (sabab yatakarrar): A recurring cause of ownership; can happen multiple times
أهل الخبرة (ahl al-khibrah): Experts who can determine whether a cause is recurring or not

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is treated as a recurring cause of ownership according to the Hanafi rule?
A) Weaving a garment that is not rewoven
B) Milking a goat
C) Renting a house
D) Shearing wool

Key Takeaway
Birth evidence and non-recurring causes take priority over absolute ownership because they establish earlier ownership; recurring causes are on par with absolute ownership and do not outweigh it.

Evidence Priority: Purchase from the Owner vs. Absolute Ownership
Rule on evidence priority when purchase is from the owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when an outsider's absolute ownership evidence conflicts with the possessor's purchase evidence and explain why purchase prevails.

Core Idea
Possessor's purchase from the owner is consistent with earlier ownership, so purchase evidence triumphs.

When two parties present conflicting ownership evidence — one proving absolute ownership and the other proving purchase from that very owner — the Hanafi school gives priority to the purchase evidence. This rule rests on the principle that a valid purchase transfer is consistent with the seller's earlier ownership, creating no contradiction. The passage sharpens this by analogy to a case where the outsider first admits the possessor's ownership then claims purchase, which would be logically inconsistent if not for the transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their respective evidence
The outsider (non-possessor) proves absolute ownership (mulk muṭlaq) with witnesses. The possessor proves purchase (shirā') from that same outsider with witnesses.
2
Understand the conflict of evidence
The outsider's evidence shows he owned the property earlier. The possessor's evidence shows he acquired it from the outsider. They appear contradictory: can the same person be both original owner and the one who sold?
3
Apply the reasoning: no contradiction
The passage states that the outsider's earlier ownership and the possessor's purchase from him are not contradictory. The possessor 'received from him' (talāqqā minhu), meaning the ownership transferred via a valid contract. Hence both sets of evidence can coexist.
4
Recognize the analogy to acknowledgment
The rule is likened to a case where the outsider first acknowledges the possessor's ownership and then claims to have purchased from him. That would be inconsistent, but here the outsider's evidence is earlier in time, not a subsequent claim. The analogy shows that purchase evidence does not negate earlier ownership; it confirms a transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجُ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ الْمُطْلَقِ وَصَاحِبُ الْيَدِ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الشَّرَاءِ مِنْهُ كَانَ صَاحِبُ الْيَدِ أولى لأنَّ الْأَوَّلَ وَإِنْ كَانَ يَثْبُتُ أَوَّلَيَّةُ الْمِلْكِ فَهَذَا تَلَقَّى مِنْهُ وَفِي هَذَا لَا تُنَافِى فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا أَقَرَّ بِالْمِلْكِ لَهُ ثُمَّ ادَّعَى الشَّرَاءَ مِنْهُ
He said: And if the outsider establishes evidence of absolute ownership, and the possessor establishes evidence of purchase from him, the possessor is more worthy, because although the first (outsider) proves priority of ownership, this (possessor) received from him, and there is no contradiction in this, so it becomes like when he (outsider) acknowledges ownership for him (possessor) and then claims purchase from him.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage gives a rule: if an outsider (non-possessor) proves absolute ownership, but the possessor proves that he purchased the item from that outsider, the possessor wins. Although the outsider proved earlier ownership, the possessor's purchase from him is a valid transfer. The analogy is made to a case where the outsider first admits the possessor's ownership and then claims to have purchased from him—which would be contradictory. But here, the outsider's earlier ownership and the subsequent purchase are consistent. So the possessor's evidence of purchase prevails.

Key Terms
شِرَاء (shirā'): Purchase; a contract of sale that transfers ownership.
مِلْك مُطْلَق (mulk muṭlaq): Absolute claim of ownership without specifying a cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why is the possessor's purchase evidence considered more worthy than the outsider's absolute ownership evidence?
A) Because the possessor is physically present.
B) Because the possessor received the property from the outsider, so there is no contradiction.
C) Because the outsider's evidence is weaker.
D) Because the possessor's evidence is newer.

Key Takeaway
When a possessor proves purchase from the same person who proves absolute ownership, the purchase evidence prevails because it is a consistent transfer.

Preference of Birth Evidence in Ownership Claims
Ruling on birth evidence with inter-madhhab dispute and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi preference rule for birth evidence in ownership disputes and evaluate the opposing positions of Ibn Abi Layla and 'Isa ibn Aban.

Core Idea
Possessor's evidence of birth outweighs outsider's ownership proof.

When two parties both dispute ownership of an animal and each brings evidence of its birth in their possession, the Hanafi school gives priority to the possessor's evidence. This ruling, supported by a hadith and rational reasoning, is contested by other scholars. This lesson unpacks the positions and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claim types
Determine who is the possessor (qaabid) and who is the outsider (khaarij). Note what each is claiming: birth evidence vs. ownership evidence.
Possession is key.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
The Hanafi ruling is that the possessor's evidence of birth is preferred. This is based on a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah recorded by Dar Qutni.
The hadith is considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
3
Examine the rational argument
Since possession does not indicate when birth occurred, the testimonies become equal. Possession then tips the scale in favor of the possessor.
Possession is a strong circumstantial indicator.
4
Contrast the opposing views
Ibn Abi Layla holds that the outsider's evidence is preferable. 'Isa ibn Aban holds that both evidences drop and the object remains with the possessor without judicial order.
These are minority positions.
5
Apply the preference rule to a new scenario
Use the rule to resolve a new case: if both parties trace ownership from a third person, the one with birth evidence wins.
The rule extends to similar non-recurring events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يدعيه الرجلان
ہے۔ جیسے نتائج یعنی کسی حیوان کے بچہ پیدا ہونا۔ روٹی کے کپڑے کی بناوٹ۔ روئی کا تنا دودھ دوہنا۔ پنیر بنانا۔ اون تراشنا وغیرہ کہ بی اسباب یک ہی بار ہوتے ہیں۔ مکرر نہیں ہوتے۔ اب شخص خارج اور قابض دونوں نے بینہ سے یہ ثابت کیا کہ یہ بچہ میرے جانور کا ہے۔ اور میری (یا میرے بائع یا میرے مورث کی ) ملک میں پیدا ہوا ہے تو قابض کا پیشہ معتبر ہوگا۔ خواہ قابض نے اپنا پینہ خارج کے حق میں قاضی کا فیصلہ ہو جانے کے بعد قائم کیا ہو یا اس سے پیشتر ۔ امام مالک اور امام احمد بھی اس کے قائل ہیں۔ اور امام شافعی کا بھی اصح قول یہی ہے۔ (ابن ابی لیلیٰ کے نزدیک شخص خارج کا ہینہ اولیٰ ہے ) ہماری نقلی دلیل دار قطنی کی روایت جابر بن عبد اللہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے ایک اونٹنی کا دعویٰ کیا اور بینہ سے ثابت کیا کہ یہ میری ہے میرے پاس جنی ہے قابض نے بھی اسی طرح بینہ سے ثابت کیا تو آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اونٹنی قابض کو دے دی۔ قوله لان السنة قامت . بیماری عقلی دلیل ہے کہ مذکورہ گواہی ایسی چیز پر قائم ہوئی ہے جس پر قبضہ دلالت نہیں کرتا تو گواہی میں دونوں برابر ہو گئے۔ پھر قابض کے بینہ کو قبضہ کی وجہ سے ترجیح ہے تو حکم اس کے لئے ہوگا۔ یہی صحیح ہے۔ عام مشائخ اسی طرف گئے ہیں۔ عیسی بن ابان اس کے خلاف ہیں۔ وہ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں پینے ساقط ہو جائیں گے۔ اور جس چیز میں جھگڑا ہے وہ قابض کے قبضہ میں بدستور چھوڑ دی جائے گی نہ کہ بطور حکم قضا۔ قوله عیسی بن ابان ... یہ علماء اصول میں سے ہیں اور قابلِ اعتماد فقیہ ہیں۔ ابن مالک کا بیان ہے کہ یہ پہلے اصحاب حدیث میں سے تھے۔ پھر ان پر رائے کا غلبہ ہو گیا۔ انہوں نے علم فقہ امام محمد سے حاصل کیا ہے۔ اور اس۲۳ھ میں وفات پائی ہے۔
ہر ایک نے ملک حاصل کی کسی شخص سے اور اسکی اپنے پاس پیدائش پر گواہ پیش کئے تو اپنے قبضہ میں پیدا ہونے پر گواہ قائم کرنے کی طرح ہے۔ ایک نے ملک پر اور دوسرے نے پیدائش پر گواہ قائم کئے تو پیدائش والا اولی ہے
وَلَوْ تَلَقَّى كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا الْمِلْكَ مِنْ رَجُلٍ وَاقَامَ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى النِّتَاجِ عِنْدَهُ فَهُوَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ إِقَامَتِهَا عَلَى النِّتَاجِ فِي يَدِ نَفْسِهِ وَلَوْ أَقَامَ اَحَدُهُمَا الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ وَالْآخَرُ عَلَى النِّتَاجِ فَصَاحِبُ النِّتَاجِ أَوْلَى أَيُّهُمَا كَانَ لِأَنَّ بِبَيِّنَتِهِ قَامَتْ عَلَى أَوَّلِيَةِ الْمِلْكِ فَلَا يَثْبُتُ الْمِلْكُ لِلْآخَرَ إِلَّا بِالتَّلَقِى مِنْ جِهَتِهِ وَكَذَلِكَ إِذَا كَانَ الدَّعْوَى بَيْنَ خَارِجِينَ فَبَيِّنَةُ النِّتَاجِ أَولَى لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا
Chapter on what two men claim. Such as birth meaning the offspring of an animal. Weaving of cloth. Spinning of cotton. Milking. Making cheese. Shearing wool etc. because these causes happen once and are not repeated. Now the outsider and the possessor both prove with evidence that this offspring is from my animal and was born in my (or my seller's or my predecessor's) ownership, then the possessor's evidence will be valid, whether the possessor presented his evidence after the judge's decision against the outsider or before. Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. And the more correct saying of Imam Shafi'i is also this. (According to Ibn Abi Layla, the outsider's evidence is preferable.) Our transmitted proof is the narration of Dar Qutni from Jabir ibn Abdullah that a man claimed a female camel and proved with evidence that it is mine, it gave birth in my possession. The possessor also proved similarly. Then the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave the camel to the possessor. His saying: Because the sunnah has been established. The rational proof is that the mentioned testimony is established on a matter that possession does not indicate, so both became equal in testimony. Then the possessor's evidence is given preference due to possession, so the ruling will be for him. This is correct. The majority of scholars have followed this. 'Isa ibn Aban is opposed to them. He says that both evidences will be dropped, and the thing in dispute will be left in the possessor's hand as is, not by way of judicial order. His saying 'Isa ibn Aban... He is from the scholars of usul and a reliable jurist. Ibn Malik states that he was from the early hadith scholars, then opinion prevailed over him. He acquired fiqh from Imam Muhammad and died in 23 AH. Each one acquired ownership from a person and presented evidence of birth in his presence, it is like presenting evidence of birth in his own hand. If one presented evidence of ownership and the other presented evidence of birth, then the one with evidence of birth is preferable. And if both trace ownership from a man and present evidence of birth with him, it is like presenting it in his own hand. If one presents evidence of ownership and the other evidence of birth, then the owner of the birth evidence is preferable, whoever he may be, because his evidence is established on the firstness of ownership, so ownership is not established for the other except by acquisition from his side. Likewise, if the claim is between two outsiders, the evidence of birth is preferable for what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on claims, discussing preference when both parties bring evidence regarding the birth of an animal or similar non-recurring events. The Hanafi position, supported by Malik, Ahmad, and the preferred view of Shafi'i, is that the possessor's evidence of birth is given preference over the outsider's evidence of absolute ownership. This is based on a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah recorded by Dar Qutni: when both the claimant and possessor brought evidence that a camel gave birth in their possession, the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled in favor of the possessor. Rationally, since possession does not indicate when birth occurred, both testimonies are equal on that point, but the possessor's possession gives his evidence precedence. Ibn Abi Layla disagrees, holding the outsider's evidence preferable, while 'Isa ibn Aban argues that both evidences drop and the thing remains with the possessor without a judicial ruling. The author follows the majority of mashayikh. The passage also includes the case where both parties trace ownership from a third party; the ruling remains the same: birth evidence outweighs absolute ownership evidence. The reason is that birth evidence establishes original ownership, while the other can only claim through acquisition from the birth owner.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitaaj): The birth of an animal (offspring)
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two witnesses
قابض (qaabid): The person in possession of the disputed object
خارج (khaarij): The outsider claiming ownership against the possessor

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when both an outsider and a possessor present evidence of birth of an animal in their respective possession, whose evidence is preferred?
A) The outsider's evidence
B) The possessor's evidence
C) Both are rejected
D) The evidence is considered equal and the property is divided

Key Takeaway
In ownership disputes over offspring, the Hanafi school prefers the possessor's evidence of birth over the outsider's evidence of ownership, a ruling supported by hadith and rational reasoning.

Accepting New Birth Evidence After Judgment
Rulings on accepting new birth evidence after judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two scenarios where new birth evidence is accepted after a judgment and state the legal reasoning (qiyas) behind each ruling.

Core Idea
Birth evidence after judgment is like a clear text overriding prior ijtihad-based rulings.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a critical question: can a judgment be overturned by later evidence? The author presents two cases involving birth evidence (bayyinah al-nitaj). In the first, a third party who was not a party to the original case can bring birth evidence to claim the property. In the second, even the party who lost based on absolute ownership can bring birth evidence to reverse the judgment. The key is the epistemological weight of birth evidence, which is compared to a clear text (nass) versus the previous judgment which is like ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (a) a third claimant after judgment for the possessor, (b) the defeated party after judgment on absolute ownership. Note the different procedural consequences.
The third claimant was not a party (maqdi 'alayh) to the previous case, so the judgment does not bind him.
2
Identify the ruling for the third claimant
The third claimant's birth evidence is accepted and judgment is given for him, unless the original possessor repeats his own evidence. The reason: the third party was not a party to the previous case.
If the possessor repeats his evidence, the case may be reopened.
3
Identify the ruling for the defeated party
If the defeated party later brings birth evidence, it is accepted and the judgment is reversed. The reason: birth evidence is like a clear text (nass) that overrides the prior judgment which was based on ijtihad.
This reversal occurs even though the defeated party was a party in the original case.
4
Analyze the qiyas
The analogy: just as a clear definitive text (nass) overrides a ruling derived by ijtihad, so birth evidence overrides the earlier judgment based on absolute ownership evidence. The illa (effective cause) is the strength and definitiveness of the evidence.
The passage explicitly states: 'this is like a text, and the first is like ijtihad.'
5
Apply the ruling to new cases
The student should be able to state the mufta bihi: both rulings are relied upon. In modern practice, this principle applies whenever new definitive evidence emerges after a judgment.
This is not a general right to appeal; only evidence of the strength of nass qualifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَضَى بِالنِّتَاجِ لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ ثُمَّ أَقَامَ ثَالِثُ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى النِّتَاحِ يُقْضَى لَهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُعِيدَهَا ذُو الْيَدِ لِاَنَّ الثَّالِثَ لَمْ يُصِرُ مَقْضِيًّا عَلَيْهِ بِتِلْكَ الْقَضِيَّةِ وَكَذَا الْمَقْضِيَ عَلَيْهِ بِالْمِلْكِ الْمُطْلَقِ إِذَا أَقَامَ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى النِّتَاجِ تُقْبَلُ وَيُنْقَضُ الْقَضَاءُ بِهِ لِأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ النَّضِ وَالْاَوَّلُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْإِجْتِهَادِ
And if judgment was given based on birth evidence for the possessor, then a third claimant presents evidence on the birth, judgment will be given for him, unless the possessor repeats his evidence, because the third claimant was not a party against whom judgment was made in that case. Similarly, the one against whom judgment was given on absolute ownership, if he presents evidence on birth, it is accepted and the judgment is thereby reversed, because this is like a text and the first is like ijtihad.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage addresses scenarios after a judgment has been made. First, if a possessor wins a case based on birth evidence, a third party can still come forward with his own birth evidence and obtain a judgment, unless the original possessor re-presents his evidence. The reason is that the third party was not a party to the previous case. Second, if a person lost a case on the basis of absolute ownership (e.g., he was ordered to hand over the property), but later he brings birth evidence, that evidence will be accepted and the earlier judgment will be overturned. The analogy is that the birth evidence is like a clear text (nass), while the earlier judgment (based on inferior evidence) is like ijtihad, which is overridden by stronger evidence.

Key Terms
مقضی علیہ (maqdi 'alayh): The party who loses the case/judgment is against them
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling or judgment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person loses a case due to absolute ownership evidence, but later brings evidence of birth, what happens to the previous judgment?
A) The judgment stands
B) The judgment is reversed
C) The new evidence is ignored
D) The case is reopened but old judgment remains

Key Takeaway
Birth evidence after a judgment is accepted if the party was not bound or if the evidence is stronger than the original proof, overriding the prior ruling like a nass overrides ijtihad.

Analogy of Weaving and Non-Recurring Causes to Birth Evidence
Extending birth evidence ruling to other non-recurring causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing recurring and non-recurring causes in ownership disputes to determine whose evidence is preferred.

Core Idea
Non-recurring causes follow birth rule; recurring causes follow absolute ownership rule.

In a dispute over ownership, the judge must decide whose evidence carries more weight. The Hanafi school has a nuanced rule based on the nature of the cause of ownership. This lesson extends the famous birth evidence ruling to other causes, clarifying when the possessor's evidence is preferred and when the outsider's evidence prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling (asl)
The base case is birth (nitaaj) of an animal: the possessor's evidence is preferred because the birth is a non-recurring, observable event that clearly links the animal to the possessor.
2
List the new cases (furu')
The passage mentions weaving a one-time garment, milking, cheese making, felting, wool shearing – all non-recurring. Also mentions recurring causes like silk weaving, building, planting, cultivation.
The key is whether the cause 'does not recur' (la yatakarraru).
3
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the cause is non-recurring, making it similar to birth in its evidentiary clarity. Recurring causes are ambiguous.
Tanqih al-manat: the recurring nature is the distinguishing factor.
4
Apply the ruling to non-recurring causes
For each non-recurring cause, the possessor's evidence is preferred by analogy to birth.
5
Handle ambiguity via experts
If it is unclear whether a cause is recurring or non-recurring, experts are consulted. If still uncertain, the default rule (outsider's evidence) applies.
This preserves the principle that the exception (birth analogy) is narrow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَكَذَلِكَ النَّسْجُ فِي القِيَابِ الَّتِي لَا تُنْسَجُ إِلَّا مَرَّةً كَغَزْلِ الْقُطْنِ وَكَذَلِكَ كُلُّ سَبَبٍ فِي الْمِلْكِ لَا يَتَكَرَّرُ لِأَنَّهُ فِي مَعْنَى النِّتَاجِ كَحَلْبِ الْلَبَنِ وَاتِّخَاذِ الْجُبْنِ وَاللَّبَدِ وَالْمَرْعِزَّى وَجَرُّ الصُّوفِ وَإِنْ كَانَ يَتَكَرَّرُ قُضِيَ بِهِ لِلْخَارِجِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمِلْكِ الْمُطْلَقِ وَهُوَ مِثْلُ الْخَرِ وَالْبِنَاءِ وَالْغَرْسِ وَزَرَاعَةِ الْحِنْطَةِ وَالْحُبُوبِ فَإِنْ أَشْكَلَ يُرْجَعُ إِلَى أَهْلِ الْخَبَرَةِ لِأَنَّهُمْ أَعْرَفُ بِهِ فَإِنْ أَشْكَلَ عَلَيْهِمْ قُضِيَ بِهِ لِلْخَارِجِ لِاَنَّ الْقَضَاءَ بِبَيِّنَتِهِ هُوَ الْأَصْلُ وَالْعُدُولُ عَنْهُ بِخَبَرِ النِّتَاجِ فَإِذَا لَمْ يَعْلَمْ يُرْجَعُ إِلَى الْأَصْلِ
He said: And likewise weaving in the garments that are only woven once, like spinning of cotton, and likewise every cause in ownership that does not recur, because it is in the meaning of birth, like milking milk, making cheese, felt, the fine wool, and shearing wool. And if it recurs, judgment is given for the outsider like absolute ownership, such as silk cloth, building, planting, cultivation of wheat and grains. If it is ambiguous, reference is made to experts because they are more knowledgeable about it. If it remains ambiguous to them, judgment is given for the outsider, because judgment based on his evidence is the original rule, and deviation from it is due to the report of birth; so when it is not known, one reverts to the original.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage extends the ruling on birth evidence to other non-recurring causes of ownership. The author (al-Quduri) states that weaving a garment that is only woven once (like cotton spinning) is analogous to birth. Similarly, milking, cheese making, felting, and shearing wool are non-recurring and thus treated like birth: the possessor's evidence is preferred. On the other hand, recurring causes like silk weaving, building, planting, and cultivating crops are considered like absolute ownership; the outsider's evidence prevails. If it is unclear whether a cause is recurring or not, experts in the field are consulted. If even they are uncertain, the default rule (outsider's evidence preferred) applies because the deviation (preferring possessor) is based on the specific knowledge of birth, which is absent.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitaaj): Birth of an animal
نسج (nasj): Weaving (of cloth)
سبب متکرر (sabab mutakarrir): Recurring cause (one that can be done multiple times, like building)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, which of the following is considered a non-recurring cause analogous to birth?
A) Building a house
B) Weaving a one-time cotton cloth
C) Planting wheat
D) Cultivating grains

Key Takeaway
Non-recurring causes of ownership are treated like birth: the possessor's evidence is preferred; recurring causes default to the outsider's evidence.

Preference of Purchase Evidence Over Absolute Ownership
Ruling on preference when outsider shows ownership and possessor shows purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an outsider proves absolute ownership and the possessor proves purchase from that outsider, and explain the reasoning using qiyas.

Core Idea
Purchase evidence from the claimed owner outweighs absolute ownership because prior ownership does not contradict later purchase.

In judicial disputes over property, it's common for an outsider to claim ownership and a possessor to claim they bought it. This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a key Hanafi ruling giving priority to the possessor's purchase evidence over the outsider's general ownership evidence, using analogy to a case of acknowledgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and proofs
Recognize the two parties: the outsider (non-possessor) who presents evidence of absolute ownership (milk mutlaq), and the possessor who presents evidence of purchase from that outsider.
Absolute ownership means ownership without specifying how it was acquired.
2
State the ruling
The possessor is more entitled (awla) to the property; his evidence is preferred over the outsider's.
The ruling is sahih (valid) and mufta bihi.
3
Understand the reasoning: no contradiction
The outsider's evidence only proves prior ownership, which does not negate a subsequent transfer. The possessor's evidence shows a lawful acquisition from the same owner, so both proofs can coexist.
The key principle: prior ownership and later purchase are not contradictory.
4
Apply the qiyas to the analogous case
The ruling is analogized to a case where a person acknowledges that the property belonged to someone else, then claims he purchased it from that person. The acknowledgment does not bar the purchase claim; similarly, the outsider's evidence of ownership does not bar the possessor's purchase claim.
The illa (effective cause) is the absence of contradiction between prior ownership and subsequent purchase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ أَقَامَ الْخَارِجُ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الْمِلْكِ الْمُطْلَقِ وَصَاحِبُ الْيَدِ الْبَيِّنَةَ عَلَى الشَّرَاءِ مِنْهُ كَانَ صَاحِبُ الْيَدِ أولى لأنَّ الْأَوَّلَ وَإِنْ كَانَ يَثْبُتُ أَوَّلَيَّةُ الْمِلْكِ فَهَذَا تَلَقَّى مِنْهُ وَفِي هَذَا لَا تُنَافِى فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا أَقَرَّ بِالْمِلْكِ لَهُ ثُمَّ ادَّعَى الشَّرَاءَ مِنْهُ
He said: And if the outsider presents evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor presents evidence of purchase from him, then the possessor is more entitled, because the first (outsider) even if it establishes precedence of ownership, this (possessor) acquired from him, and in this there is no contradiction, so it becomes like when he acknowledged ownership for him and then claimed purchase from him.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where an outsider (non-possessor) proves absolute ownership of an item, but the current possessor proves that he purchased it from that outsider. The ruling is that the possessor is entitled to keep the item. The reason is that the outsider's evidence may show he once owned it, but the possessor's evidence shows he acquired ownership from that outsider. There is no contradiction because the outsider could have sold it. The ruling is analogized to a case where a person acknowledges that the item belonged to someone else and then claims he purchased it from that person; the acknowledgment does not negate the possibility of subsequent purchase.

Key Terms
شراء (shira'): Purchase, buying
ملک مطلق (milk mutlaq): Absolute ownership without specifying cause

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, when an outsider proves absolute ownership and the possessor proves purchase from that outsider, who is entitled to the property?
A) The outsider
B) The possessor
C) Both share equally
D) The property is divided based on the value of evidence

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, purchase evidence from the claimed owner outweighs absolute ownership evidence because prior ownership and later purchase can coexist.

Priority of Birth Evidence over Absolute Ownership
Ruling that birth evidence overrides absolute ownership evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that birth evidence takes precedence over absolute ownership evidence and explain the analogy to نَصّ versus ijtihad.

Core Idea
Birth evidence is like نَصّ; absolute ownership is like ijtihad – the specific overrides the general.

In ownership disputes, not all evidence is equal. The Hanafi school distinguishes between evidence that specifies a cause, such as birth (نتاج) from one's own property, and evidence that merely asserts ownership without a cause (ملک مطلق). This lesson explores why the former is given decisive weight, drawing a powerful analogy to the relationship between نَصّ (definitive text) and ijtihad in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
Recognize that one claimant brings evidence of absolute ownership (ملك مطلق), while the other brings evidence that the property was born from his own owned animal or slave (نتاج).
The property could be a slave or livestock.
2
State the ruling
The Hanafi ruling: the evidence of birth (نتاج) is given precedence over evidence of absolute ownership, regardless of any other circumstances.
This is a mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the analogy
The commentator compares birth evidence to a definitive text (نص) and absolute ownership evidence to ijtihad. Just as a نص overrides ijtihad, birth evidence overrides general ownership claims.
This analogy is specific to this fiqhi context and not a general usul principle.
4
Apply the ruling
If a judgment has already been made based on absolute ownership, and the defeated party later brings birth evidence, the first judgment is overturned.
This shows the strength of birth evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولو تلقى كل واحد .. اگر قابض میں سے ہر ایک نے کسی شخص کی طرف سے حصول ملک کا دعوی کر کے اس امر پر بینے قائم کئے کہ یہ چیز اس کے پاس اس کی مملوکہ چیز سے پیدا ہوئی ہے۔ مثلاً زید کے قبضہ میں ایک غلام ہے۔ اس پر بکر نے دعوے کیا کہ میں نے یہ غلام خالد سے خریدا ہے جو خالد کی ملک میں اس کی مملوکہ باندی سے پیدا ہوا تھا۔ اور قابض (زید) نے ہینہ قائم کیا۔ کہ میں نے یہ غلام عمر و سے خریدا ہے جو اس کی ملک میں اس کی مملوکہ باندی سے پیدا ہوا تھا۔ تو قابض کو تربیج ہو کر حکم اس کے لئے ہوگا۔

قوله ولو قضى بالنتاج .. اگر قابض کے لئے بذریعہ نتاج حکم دے دیا گیا پھر تیسرے مدعی نے بہینہ قائم کیا کہ یہ میری مملوکہ سے میری ملک میں پیدا ہوا ہے۔ تو تیسرے کے لئے حکم دے دیا جائے گا۔ مگر یہ کہ قابض اپنابینہ دوبارہ پیش کرے۔ اس لئے کہ قابض کے واسطے حکم ہو جانے میں تیسرا مدعی تحت القضا داخل نہیں ہوا یعنی اس پر یہ حکم نہیں ہوا کہ اس کا دعوئی خارج ہے۔ اسی طرح جو شخص ملک مطلق کے دعوئی میں مقتضی علیہ ہو جائے یعنی وہ خارج کر دیا جائے اور مدعی کے لئے حکم ہو جائے پھر وہ نتاج پر بینہ لائے تو قبول ہوگا۔ اور اس کے ذریعہ سے حکم اول تو ڑ دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ نتاج کا حکم بمنزلہ نص ہے اور اول بمنزلہ اجتہاد اور مجتہد کو جب اس کے اجتہاد کے خلاف نص مل جائے تو حکم اول ٹوٹ کر نص کے موافق حکم ہوتا ہے تو ایسے ہی یہاں ہوگا۔

فائدہ ... مقضی علیہ بملک مطلق کی مثال یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص کے قبضہ میں ایک چوپایہ ہے اس پر زید نے ملک مطلق کا دعوی کر کے ہینہ قائم کیا جس کے نتیجہ میں چوپایہ زید کو دلا دیا گیا۔ پس وہ شخص مقضی علیہ اور زید مقضی لہ ہے۔ اب اگر قابض نتاج پر ہینہ قائم کرنے تو مقبول ہو گا اور زید کے لئے جو حکم ہو تھا وہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔ قوله وان اقام الخارج مشخص خارج ( غیر قابض) نے ملک مطلق پر ہینہ قائم کیا اور قابض نے اس بات پر کہ میں نے شخص خارج سے خریدا ہے تو قابض کا بینہ معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مشخص خارج اولیت ملک کو ثابت کر رہا ہے۔ اور قابض اس سے ملک حاصل کر دونوں میں کوئی منافات نہیں تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے قابض نے پہلے غیر کے لئے ملکیت کا اقرار کیا۔ پھر اس سے خرید لینے کا دعویٰ کیا۔
Translation: If each of them claims acquisition of ownership from a person and establishes evidence that it was born in his possession, then it is like establishing evidence of birth in his own possession. If one establishes evidence of ownership and the other of birth, the one with birth evidence is preferred, regardless, because his evidence is prior in establishing ownership, so ownership is not established for the other except through his direction. Similarly, if the claim is between two non-possessors, the evidence of birth is preferred. If judgment has been given [to the possessor], and the possessor presents evidence again, because the third claimant was not a party to the judgment. Similarly, the one against whom a judgment of absolute ownership has been passed, if he brings evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the first judgment will be overtured, because this is like a definitive text (nas) and the first is like ijtihad. Similarly, for items that are not manufactured repeatedly, like cotton cloth, and every cause of ownership that is not repetitive, because it is in the sense of birth (natal), like milking, cheese-making, felting, shearing wool, trimming goat hair. And if it is repetitive, then judgment will be for the non-possessor, like absolute ownership, e.g., wool cloth, building construction, planting, cultivating wheat and grains. Then if there is ambiguity, experts are consulted. If they remain ambiguous, judgment is for the non-possessor, because the original principle is that judgment is given on his evidence, and deviation from that was due to the nature of birth; when that is unknown, we revert to the original. If a non-possessor establishes evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor establishes evidence of purchase from him, the possessor is preferred, because although the first established priority of ownership, the possessor established acquisition from him, and there is no contradiction. So it is as if the possessor acknowledged ownership for the other and then claimed purchase from him. Explanation: His statement 'And if each one receives...': If each of the possessors claims acquisition of ownership from a person and establishes evidence that this thing was born from his owned property in his possession, e.g., Zayd has a slave in his possession, and Bakr claims, 'I bought this slave from Khalid, and he was born from a slave-girl owned by Khalid,' and the possessor (Zayd) establishes evidence that he bought the slave from 'Amr, who owned the slave-girl, then the possessor is given preference and judgment is for him. His statement 'And if judgment was given by birth...': If judgment was given for the possessor by means of birth, then a third claimant brings evidence that it was born from his own owned property in his ownership, then judgment will be given for the third, unless the possessor presents his evidence again. Because the third claimant was not under the judgment that his claim is rejected. Similarly, a person who was made the defendant in a case of absolute ownership (i.e., he was dispossessed) and judgment was given for the claimant, then if he brings evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the first judgment will be overtured, because the ruling of birth is like a text (nas) and the first is like ijtihad. When a mujtahid finds a text contrary to his ijtihad, the first judgment is broken and a judgment in accordance with the text is given. So here too. Benefit: Example of a defendant in absolute ownership: A person has an animal in his possession, and Zayd claims absolute ownership and establishes evidence, resulting in the animal being given to Zayd. So that person is the defendant and Zayd is the claimant. Now if the possessor establishes evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the judgment for Zayd will be overtured. His statement 'And if the outsider establishes...': If the outsider (non-possessor) establishes evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor establishes that he bought from that outsider, then the possessor's evidence is considered, because the outsider is proving priority of ownership, and the possessor is proving acquisition from him, and there is no contradiction. So it is as if the possessor first acknowledged ownership for the outsider and then claimed purchase from him.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the priority of evidence when multiple parties claim ownership of a property. The opening line identifies the chapter on what two men claim (باب ما يدعيه الرجلان). The commentator explains the vocabulary, then translates the ruling. The core principle is that evidence of birth (نتاج) — meaning that the property was born from an animal or slave owned by the claimant — is considered stronger than evidence of absolute ownership (ملک مطلق). This is because birth evidence is like a definitive text (نص) that leaves no room for ijtihad, whereas absolute ownership claims are based on ijtihad. Therefore, if a person was defeated in a case of absolute ownership, they can later bring evidence of birth and overturn the previous judgment. The commentary then extends this analogy to non-repetitive causes of ownership like milking, cheese-making, etc., versus repetitive causes like building, planting, and cultivating. If there is doubt, experts are consulted; if even experts cannot decide, the original principle (judgment for the non-possessor) prevails. The passage also covers the scenario where a non-possessor claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from that non-possessor — here the possessor is preferred because the two claims are not contradictory; the possessor is essentially acknowledging the other's ownership and then claiming a transfer from him.

Key Terms
نتاج (nataj): Birth of an animal or slave; used to denote property that is born from owned livestock or slaves.
ملک مطلق (mulk mutlaq): Absolute ownership claimed without specifying a cause.
نص (nass): A definitive text from Quran or Sunnah that leaves no room for interpretation; here used analogically for birth evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a disputer over ownership, one party presents evidence of birth (نتاج) and the other presents evidence of absolute ownership (ملک مطلق). Which evidence takes precedence according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Absolute ownership evidence
B) Birth evidence
C) They are equal
D) The judge decides by lot

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi judicial practice, evidence of a specific cause of ownership (like birth) overrides general claims of ownership, analogous to the overriding authority of نص over ijtihad.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Non-Repetitive Causes Analogous to Birth
Extension of birth evidence priority to non-repetitive causes of ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify non-repetitive causes of ownership (like milking, cheese-making) and apply the same evidentiary priority as birth evidence.

Core Idea
Any non-repetitive cause of ownership is treated like birth evidence in disputes.

The principle that birth evidence overrides general ownership is extended to other causes that are non-repetitive (غير مكرر), such as milking, cheese-making, felting, and shearing. These are considered similar to birth because they cannot be repeated on the same property. This lesson clarifies the analogy and scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify non-repetitive causes
List the examples given: milking, cheese-making, felting, shearing wool, trimming goat hair.
These are called non-repetitive because once done, the original material is transformed or consumed.
2
Link to birth evidence
The commentator states these are 'in the same sense as birth' (فإنه في معنى النتاج).
Thus they receive the same evidentiary weight.
3
Apply the ruling
In a dispute, if one party proves they produced the item from their own property through such a non-repetitive process, their evidence overrides a general ownership claim.
The ruling is the same as for birth.

بنانا، نمدہ بنانا، اون کا شنا، بھیڑی کا رواں تراشنا۔ اور اگر مکرر ہوتا ہو تو غیر قابض کے لئے حکم ہو جائے گا۔ بمنزلہ ملک مطلق کے جیسے اون کا کپڑا بناء عمارت بنان، پودہ لگانا، گیہوں اور اناج کی کاشت اس سے عدول خبر نتاج کی وجہ سے تھا۔ اور جب یہ معلوم نہیں ہوا تو اصل کی طرف رجوع کیا جائے گا۔ اگر ہیتہ قائم کیا غیر قابض نے ملک مطلق پر اور قابض نے اس سے خریدنے پر تو قابض اولی ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ اول نے Translation: If each of them claims acquisition of ownership from a person and establishes evidence that it was born in his possession, then it is like establishing evidence of birth in his own possession. If one establishes evidence of ownership and the other of birth, the one with birth evidence is preferred, regardless, because his evidence is prior in establishing ownership, so ownership is not established for the other except through his direction. Similarly, if the claim is between two non-possessors, the evidence of birth is preferred. If judgment has been given [to the possessor], and the possessor presents evidence again, because the third claimant was not a party to the judgment. Similarly, the one against whom a judgment of absolute ownership has been passed, if he brings evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the first judgment will be overtured, because this is like a definitive text (nas) and the first is like ijtihad. Similarly, for items that are not manufactured repeatedly, like cotton cloth, and every cause of ownership that is not repetitive, because it is in the sense of birth (natal), like milking, cheese-making, felting, shearing wool, trimming goat hair. And if it is repetitive, then judgment will be for the non-possessor, like absolute ownership, e.g., wool cloth, building construction, planting, cultivating wheat and grains. Then if there is ambiguity, experts are consulted. If they remain ambiguous, judgment is for the non-possessor, because the original principle is that judgment is given on his evidence, and deviation from that was due to the nature of birth; when that is unknown, we revert to the original. If a non-possessor establishes evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor establishes evidence of purchase from him, the possessor is preferred, because although the first established priority of ownership, the possessor established acquisition from him, and there is no contradiction. So it is as if the possessor acknowledged ownership for the other and then claimed purchase from him. Explanation: His statement 'And if each one receives...': If each of the possessors claims acquisition of ownership from a person and establishes evidence that this thing was born from his owned property in his possession, e.g., Zayd has a slave in his possession, and Bakr claims, 'I bought this slave from Khalid, and he was born from a slave-girl owned by Khalid,' and the possessor (Zayd) establishes evidence that he bought the slave from 'Amr, who owned the slave-girl, then the possessor is given preference and judgment is for him. His statement 'And if judgment was given by birth...': If judgment was given for the possessor by means of birth, then a third claimant brings evidence that it was born from his own owned property in his ownership, then judgment will be given for the third, unless the possessor presents his evidence again. Because the third claimant was not under the judgment that his claim is rejected. Similarly, a person who was made the defendant in a case of absolute ownership (i.e., he was dispossessed) and judgment was given for the claimant, then if he brings evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the first judgment will be overtured, because the ruling of birth is like a text (nas) and the first is like ijtihad. When a mujtahid finds a text contrary to his ijtihad, the first judgment is broken and a judgment in accordance with the text is given. So here too. Benefit: Example of a defendant in absolute ownership: A person has an animal in his possession, and Zayd claims absolute ownership and establishes evidence, resulting in the animal being given to Zayd. So that person is the defendant and Zayd is the claimant. Now if the possessor establishes evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the judgment for Zayd will be overtured. His statement 'And if the outsider establishes...': If the outsider (non-possessor) establishes evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor establishes that he bought from that outsider, then the possessor's evidence is considered, because the outsider is proving priority of ownership, and the possessor is proving acquisition from him, and there is no contradiction. So it is as if the possessor first acknowledged ownership for the outsider and then claimed purchase from him.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the priority of evidence when multiple parties claim ownership of a property. The opening line identifies the chapter on what two men claim (باب ما يدعيه الرجلان). The commentator explains the vocabulary, then translates the ruling. The core principle is that evidence of birth (نتاج) — meaning that the property was born from an animal or slave owned by the claimant — is considered stronger than evidence of absolute ownership (ملک مطلق). This is because birth evidence is like a definitive text (نص) that leaves no room for ijtihad, whereas absolute ownership claims are based on ijtihad. Therefore, if a person was defeated in a case of absolute ownership, they can later bring evidence of birth and overturn the previous judgment. The commentary then extends this analogy to non-repetitive causes of ownership like milking, cheese-making, etc., versus repetitive causes like building, planting, and cultivating. If there is doubt, experts are consulted; if even experts cannot decide, the original principle (judgment for the non-possessor) prevails. The passage also covers the scenario where a non-possessor claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from that non-possessor — here the possessor is preferred because the two claims are not contradictory; the possessor is essentially acknowledging the other's ownership and then claiming a transfer from him.

Key Terms
سبب غير مكرر (sabab ghayr mukarrar): A cause of ownership that occurs only once on a given property, such as birth, milking, shearing, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered a non-repetitive cause analogous to birth?
A) Building a house
B) Planting wheat
C) Shearing wool
D) Manufacturing cloth

Key Takeaway
Non-repetitive causes of ownership (e.g., milking, shearing) are given the same evidentiary weight as birth evidence, overriding general ownership claims.

Qada — Judiciary

Repetitive Causes and the Original Principle
Ruling on repetitive causes and return to original principle when ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish repetitive causes (like building, planting) and state that the original principle (judgment for non-possessor) applies when the cause is repetitive or ambiguous.

Core Idea
Repetitive causes are like absolute ownership; if ambiguous, revert to original principle.

Not all causes are non-repetitive. Building a house, planting crops, and weaving cloth are repetitive – they can be done repeatedly. The Hanafi rule treats evidence of repetitive causes like evidence of absolute ownership: it does not get birth-like priority. If there is doubt about the nature of the cause, experts are consulted; if they remain uncertain, the original principle that judgment is for the non-possessor prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify repetitive causes
Examples: wool cloth (since it can be woven repeatedly), building construction, planting trees, cultivating wheat and grains.
These are activities that can be done again on the same or similar material.
2
State the ruling for repetitive causes
Such causes are treated like absolute ownership (ملك مطلق); the evidence does not override, and judgment is for the non-possessor (if other conditions are equal).
This is the default rule.
3
Handle ambiguity
If the parties disagree on whether the cause is repetitive or non-repetitive, experts (أهل الخبرة) are consulted. If the experts remain uncertain, we revert to the original principle: judgment for the non-possessor.
The original principle is that the non-possessor's evidence is the basis (الأصل).

ولو تلقى كل واحد .. اگر قابض میں سے ہر ایک نے کسی شخص کی طرف سے حصول ملک کا دعوی کر کے اس امر پر بینے قائم کئے کہ یہ چیز اس کے پاس اس کی مملوکہ چیز سے پیدا ہوئی ہے۔ مثلاً زید کے قبضہ میں ایک غلام ہے۔ اس پر بکر نے دعوے کیا کہ میں نے یہ غلام خالد سے خریدا ہے جو خالد کی ملک میں اس کی مملوکہ باندی سے پیدا ہوا تھا۔ اور قابض (زید) نے ہینہ قائم کیا۔ کہ میں نے یہ غلام عمر و سے خریدا ہے جو اس کی ملک میں اس کی مملوکہ باندی سے پیدا ہوا تھا۔ تو قابض کو تربیج ہو کر حکم اس کے لئے ہوگا۔

قوله ولو قضى بالنتاج .. اگر قابض کے لئے بذریعہ نتاج حکم دے دیا گیا پھر تیسرے مدعی نے بہینہ قائم کیا کہ یہ میری مملوکہ سے میری ملک میں پیدا ہوا ہے۔ تو تیسرے کے لئے حکم دے دیا جائے گا۔ مگر یہ کہ قابض اپنابینہ دوبارہ پیش کرے۔ اس لئے کہ قابض کے واسطے حکم ہو جانے میں تیسرا مدعی تحت القضا داخل نہیں ہوا یعنی اس پر یہ حکم نہیں ہوا کہ اس کا دعوئی خارج ہے۔ اسی طرح جو شخص ملک مطلق کے دعوئی میں مقتضی علیہ ہو جائے یعنی وہ خارج کر دیا جائے اور مدعی کے لئے حکم ہو جائے پھر وہ نتاج پر بینہ لائے تو قبول ہوگا۔ اور اس کے ذریعہ سے حکم اول تو ڑ دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ نتاج کا حکم بمنزلہ نص ہے اور اول بمنزلہ اجتہاد اور مجتہد کو جب اس کے اجتہاد کے خلاف نص مل جائے تو حکم اول ٹوٹ کر نص کے موافق حکم ہوتا ہے تو ایسے ہی یہاں ہوگا۔

فائدہ ... مقضی علیہ بملک مطلق کی مثال یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص کے قبضہ میں ایک چوپایہ ہے اس پر زید نے ملک مطلق کا دعوی کر کے ہینہ قائم کیا جس کے نتیجہ میں چوپایہ زید کو دلا دیا گیا۔ پس وہ شخص مقضی علیہ اور زید مقضی لہ ہے۔ اب اگر قابض نتاج پر ہینہ قائم کرنے تو مقبول ہو گا اور زید کے لئے جو حکم ہو تھا وہ ٹوٹ جائے گا۔ قوله وان اقام الخارج مشخص خارج ( غیر قابض) نے ملک مطلق پر ہینہ قائم کیا اور قابض نے اس بات پر کہ میں نے شخص خارج سے خریدا ہے تو قابض کا بینہ معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مشخص خارج اولیت ملک کو ثابت کر رہا ہے۔ اور قابض اس سے ملک حاصل کر دونوں میں کوئی منافات نہیں تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے قابض نے پہلے غیر کے لئے ملکیت کا اقرار کیا۔ پھر اس سے خرید لینے کا دعویٰ کیا۔
Translation: If each of them claims acquisition of ownership from a person and establishes evidence that it was born in his possession, then it is like establishing evidence of birth in his own possession. If one establishes evidence of ownership and the other of birth, the one with birth evidence is preferred, regardless, because his evidence is prior in establishing ownership, so ownership is not established for the other except through his direction. Similarly, if the claim is between two non-possessors, the evidence of birth is preferred. If judgment has been given [to the possessor], and the possessor presents evidence again, because the third claimant was not a party to the judgment. Similarly, the one against whom a judgment of absolute ownership has been passed, if he brings evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the first judgment will be overtured, because this is like a definitive text (nas) and the first is like ijtihad. Similarly, for items that are not manufactured repeatedly, like cotton cloth, and every cause of ownership that is not repetitive, because it is in the sense of birth (natal), like milking, cheese-making, felting, shearing wool, trimming goat hair. And if it is repetitive, then judgment will be for the non-possessor, like absolute ownership, e.g., wool cloth, building construction, planting, cultivating wheat and grains. Then if there is ambiguity, experts are consulted. If they remain ambiguous, judgment is for the non-possessor, because the original principle is that judgment is given on his evidence, and deviation from that was due to the nature of birth; when that is unknown, we revert to the original. If a non-possessor establishes evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor establishes evidence of purchase from him, the possessor is preferred, because although the first established priority of ownership, the possessor established acquisition from him, and there is no contradiction. So it is as if the possessor acknowledged ownership for the other and then claimed purchase from him. Explanation: His statement 'And if each one receives...': If each of the possessors claims acquisition of ownership from a person and establishes evidence that this thing was born from his owned property in his possession, e.g., Zayd has a slave in his possession, and Bakr claims, 'I bought this slave from Khalid, and he was born from a slave-girl owned by Khalid,' and the possessor (Zayd) establishes evidence that he bought the slave from 'Amr, who owned the slave-girl, then the possessor is given preference and judgment is for him. His statement 'And if judgment was given by birth...': If judgment was given for the possessor by means of birth, then a third claimant brings evidence that it was born from his own owned property in his ownership, then judgment will be given for the third, unless the possessor presents his evidence again. Because the third claimant was not under the judgment that his claim is rejected. Similarly, a person who was made the defendant in a case of absolute ownership (i.e., he was dispossessed) and judgment was given for the claimant, then if he brings evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the first judgment will be overtured, because the ruling of birth is like a text (nas) and the first is like ijtihad. When a mujtahid finds a text contrary to his ijtihad, the first judgment is broken and a judgment in accordance with the text is given. So here too. Benefit: Example of a defendant in absolute ownership: A person has an animal in his possession, and Zayd claims absolute ownership and establishes evidence, resulting in the animal being given to Zayd. So that person is the defendant and Zayd is the claimant. Now if the possessor establishes evidence of birth, it will be accepted and the judgment for Zayd will be overtured. His statement 'And if the outsider establishes...': If the outsider (non-possessor) establishes evidence of absolute ownership and the possessor establishes that he bought from that outsider, then the possessor's evidence is considered, because the outsider is proving priority of ownership, and the possessor is proving acquisition from him, and there is no contradiction. So it is as if the possessor first acknowledged ownership for the outsider and then claimed purchase from him.
